<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Linda Ronstadt</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>

<p><b>Linda Maria Ronstadt</b> (born July 15, 1946) is an American <a href="/wiki/Popular_music" title="Popular music">popular music</a> singer. She has earned 11 <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Award" title="Grammy Award">Grammy Awards</a>, three <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_Music_Awards" title="American Music Awards">American Music Awards</a>, two <a href="/wiki/Academy_of_Country_Music" title="Academy of Country Music">Academy of Country Music</a> awards, an <a href="/wiki/Emmy_Award" title="Emmy Award">Emmy Award</a>, and an <a href="/wiki/ALMA_Award" title="ALMA Award">ALMA Award</a>, and many of her albums have been <a href="/wiki/Music_recording_sales_certification" title="Music recording sales certification">certified gold, platinum or multiplatinum</a> in the United states and internationally. She has also earned nominations for a <a href="/wiki/Tony_Award" title="Tony Award">Tony Award</a> and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Golden_Globe" title="Golden Globe">Golden Globe</a> award. She was inducted into the <a href="/wiki/Rock_and_Roll_Hall_of_Fame" title="Rock and Roll Hall of Fame">Rock and Roll Hall of Fame</a> in April 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> On July 28, 2014, she was awarded the National Medal of Arts and Humanities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>In total, she has released over 30 studio albums and 15 <a href="/wiki/Compilation_album" title="Compilation album">compilation</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greatest_hits" title="Greatest hits">greatest hits</a> albums. Ronstadt charted 38 <a href="/wiki/Billboard_Hot_100" title="Billboard Hot 100"><i>Billboard</i> Hot 100</a> singles, with 21 reaching the top 40, 10 in the top 10, three at number 2, and "<a href="/wiki/You%27re_No_Good" title="You're No Good">You're No Good</a>" at number 1. This success did not translate to the UK, with only her single "<a href="/wiki/Blue_Bayou#Ronstadt_version" title="Blue Bayou">Blue Bayou</a>" reaching the UK Top 40.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Her duet with <a href="/wiki/Aaron_Neville" title="Aaron Neville">Aaron Neville</a>, "<a href="/wiki/Don%27t_Know_Much" title="Don't Know Much">Don't Know Much</a>", peaked at number 2 in December 1989.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> In addition, she has charted 36 albums, 10 top-10 albums and three number 1 albums on the <i>Billboard</i> Pop Album Chart. Her autobiography, <i>Simple Dreams: A Musical Memoir</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Memoir_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Memoir-20">[20]</a></sup> was published in September 2013. It debuted in the Top 10 on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times_Best_Sellers_List" title="The New York Times Best Sellers List">New York Times Best Sellers List</a>.</p>
<p>Ronstadt has collaborated with artists in diverse genres, including <a href="/wiki/Bette_Midler" title="Bette Midler">Bette Midler</a>, <a href="/wiki/Billy_Eckstine" title="Billy Eckstine">Billy Eckstine</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eckstine_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eckstine-21">[21]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Frank_Zappa" title="Frank Zappa">Frank Zappa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rosemary_Clooney" title="Rosemary Clooney">Rosemary Clooney</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flaco_Jim%C3%A9nez" title="Flaco Jiménez">Flaco Jiménez</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philip_Glass" title="Philip Glass">Philip Glass</a>, <a href="/wiki/Warren_Zevon" title="Warren Zevon">Warren Zevon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emmylou_Harris" title="Emmylou Harris">Emmylou Harris</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gram_Parsons" title="Gram Parsons">Gram Parsons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dolly_Parton" title="Dolly Parton">Dolly Parton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neil_Young" title="Neil Young">Neil Young</a>, <a href="/wiki/Johnny_Cash" title="Johnny Cash">Johnny Cash</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nelson_Riddle" title="Nelson Riddle">Nelson Riddle</a>. She has lent her voice to over 120 albums and has sold more than 100 million records, making her one of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_best-selling_music_artists" title="List of best-selling music artists">world's best-selling artists of all time</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guest_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Guest-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Christopher Loudon, of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jazz_Times" title="Jazz Times">Jazz Times</a></i>, wrote in 2004 that Ronstadt is "blessed with arguably the most sterling set of pipes of her generation."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JazzTimes2_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-JazzTimes2-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>After completing her last live concert in late 2009, Ronstadt retired in 2011. She was diagnosed as having <a href="/wiki/Parkinson%27s_disease" title="Parkinson's disease">Parkinson's disease</a> in December 2012, which left her unable to sing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linda_Ronstadt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Linda Maria Ronstadt was born in 1946 in <a href="/wiki/Tucson,_Arizona" title="Tucson, Arizona">Tucson, Arizona</a>, daughter to Gilbert Ronstadt (1911–1995), a prosperous machinery merchant who ran the F. Ronstadt Co.,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fredronstadt_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-fredronstadt-26">[26]</a></sup> and Ruth Mary (Copeman) Ronstadt (1914–1982), a homemaker.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>Ronstadt was raised on the family's 10-acre (4.0 ha) ranch with her siblings Peter (who served as Tucson's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chief_of_Police" title="Chief of Police">Chief of Police</a> for ten years, 1981–1991), Michael J., and Gretchen (Suzy). The family was featured in <i><a href="/wiki/Family_Circle" title="Family Circle">Family Circle</a></i> magazine in 1953.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-family_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-family-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>Linda's father came from a <a href="/wiki/Settler" title="Settler">pioneering</a> Arizona ranching family<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tiptheirhat_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-tiptheirhat-29">[29]</a></sup> and was of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_American" title="German American">German</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_American" title="English American">English</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mexican_people" title="Mexican people">Mexican</a> ancestry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tucsonsronstadtfamilyhistory_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-tucsonsronstadtfamilyhistory-30">[30]</a></sup> The family's influence on and contributions to Arizona's history, including wagon making, commerce, pharmacies, and music, are chronicled in the library of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Arizona" title="University of Arizona">University of Arizona</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tucsonsronstadtfamily_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-tucsonsronstadtfamily-31">[31]</a></sup> Linda Ronstadt's great-grandfather, graduate engineer Friedrich August Ronstadt (who went by Federico Augusto Ronstadt) emigrated to the <a href="/wiki/Southwestern_United_States" title="Southwestern United States">Southwest</a> (then a part of Mexico) in the 1840s from <a href="/wiki/Hanover" title="Hanover">Hanover</a>, Germany, and married a Mexican citizen, eventually settling in Tucson.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-helmut_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-helmut-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lindar1_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-lindar1-33">[33]</a></sup> In 1991, the City of Tucson opened its central transit terminal on March 16 and dedicated it to Linda's grandfather, <a href="/wiki/Federico_Jos%C3%A9_Mar%C3%ADa_Ronstadt" title="Federico José María Ronstadt">Federico José María Ronstadt</a>, a local pioneer businessman; he was a wagon maker whose early contribution to the city's mobility included six mule-drawn streetcars delivered in 1903–04.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fredronstadt2_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-fredronstadt2-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>Her mother Ruth Mary, of German, English, and <a href="/wiki/Dutch_people" title="Dutch people">Dutch</a> ancestry, was raised in <a href="/wiki/Flint,_Michigan" title="Flint, Michigan">Flint, Michigan</a>. She was a daughter of <a href="/wiki/Lloyd_Groff_Copeman" title="Lloyd Groff Copeman">Lloyd Groff Copeman</a>, a prolific inventor and holder of many patents. Copeman, with nearly 700 patents to his name, invented an early form of the toaster, many refrigerator devices, the grease gun, the first electric stove, and an early form of the microwave oven. His flexible rubber ice cube tray earned him millions of dollars in royalties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LloydCopeman_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-LloydCopeman-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Career_summary">Career summary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linda_Ronstadt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Career summary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: left; clear: left; margin: 0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0; width:25%; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>"Everybody has their own level of doing their music. ... Mine just happened to resonate over the years, in one way and another, with a significant enough number of people so that I could do it professionally."</p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">—Linda Ronstadt<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldmine589_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goldmine589-36">[36]</a></sup></cite></div>
<p>Establishing her professional career in the mid-1960s at the forefront of California's emerging <a href="/wiki/Folk_rock" title="Folk rock">folk rock</a> and <a href="/wiki/Country_rock" title="Country rock">country rock</a> movements – genres which defined post-1960s rock music – Ronstadt joined forces with <a href="/wiki/Bobby_Kimmel" title="Bobby Kimmel">Bobby Kimmel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kenny_Edwards" title="Kenny Edwards">Kenny Edwards</a> and became the lead singer of a folk-rock trio, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Stone_Poneys" title="The Stone Poneys">Stone Poneys</a>. Later, as a solo artist, she released <i>Hand Sown ... Home Grown</i> in 1969, which has been described as the first <a href="/wiki/Alternative_country" title="Alternative country">alternative country</a> record by a female recording artist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Linen106_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Linen106-37">[37]</a></sup> Although fame eluded her during these years, Ronstadt actively toured with the <a href="/wiki/The_Doors" title="The Doors">Doors</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neil_Young" title="Neil Young">Neil Young</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jackson_Browne" title="Jackson Browne">Jackson Browne</a>, and others, appeared numerous times on television shows, and began to contribute her singing to albums by other artists.</p>
<p>With the release of chart-topping albums such as <i><a href="/wiki/Heart_Like_a_Wheel" title="Heart Like a Wheel">Heart Like a Wheel</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Simple_Dreams" title="Simple Dreams">Simple Dreams</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Living_in_the_USA" title="Living in the USA">Living in the USA</a></i>, Ronstadt became the first female "arena class" rock star. She set records as one of the top-grossing concert artists of the decade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Linen106_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-Linen106-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-doubleplatinum_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-doubleplatinum-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RocksVenusSuccess_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-RocksVenusSuccess-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hamill_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-hamill-40">[40]</a></sup> Referred to as the "First Lady of Rock"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tiptheirhat_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-tiptheirhat-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> and the "Queen of Rock", Ronstadt was voted the Top Female Pop Singer of the 1970s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tiptheirhat_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-tiptheirhat-29">[29]</a></sup> Her rock-and-roll image was as famous as her music; she appeared six times on the cover of <i><a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone" title="Rolling Stone">Rolling Stone</a></i> and on the covers of <i><a href="/wiki/Newsweek" title="Newsweek">Newsweek</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i>.</p>
<p>In the 1980s, Ronstadt went to Broadway and garnered a Tony nomination for her performance in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Pirates_of_Penzance" title="The Pirates of Penzance">The Pirates of Penzance</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> teamed with the composer <a href="/wiki/Philip_Glass" title="Philip Glass">Philip Glass</a>, recorded traditional music, and collaborated with the conductor <a href="/wiki/Nelson_Riddle" title="Nelson Riddle">Nelson Riddle</a>, an event at that time viewed as an original and unorthodox move for a rock-and-roll artist. This venture paid off,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gambleoff_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-gambleoff-43">[43]</a></sup> and Ronstadt remained one of the music industry's best-selling acts throughout the 1980s, with multi-platinum-selling albums such as <i><a href="/wiki/What%27s_New_(Linda_Ronstadt_album)" title="What's New (Linda Ronstadt album)">What's New</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Canciones_de_Mi_Padre" title="Canciones de Mi Padre">Canciones de Mi Padre</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Cry_Like_a_Rainstorm,_Howl_Like_the_Wind" title="Cry Like a Rainstorm, Howl Like the Wind">Cry Like a Rainstorm, Howl Like the Wind</a></i>. She continued to tour, collaborate, and record celebrated albums, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Winter_Light_(Linda_Ronstadt_album)" title="Winter Light (Linda Ronstadt album)">Winter Light</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Hummin%27_to_Myself_(Linda_Ronstadt_album)" title="Hummin' to Myself (Linda Ronstadt album)">Hummin' to Myself</a></i>, until her retirement in 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-retirement_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-retirement-44">[44]</a></sup> Most of Ronstadt's albums are <a href="/wiki/Music_recording_sales_certification" title="Music recording sales certification">certified</a> gold, platinum, or multi-platinum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-certifications_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-certifications-46">[46]</a></sup> Having sold in excess of 100 million records worldwide<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-worldwidesales_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-worldwidesales-47">[47]</a></sup> and setting records as one of the top-grossing concert performers for over a decade, Ronstadt was the most successful female singer of the 1970s and stands as one of the most successful female recording artists in U.S. history. A consummate American artist, Ronstadt opened many doors for women in rock and roll and other musical genres by championing songwriters and musicians, pioneering her chart success onto the concert circuit, and being at the vanguard of many musical movements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Linen106_37-2"><a href="#cite_note-Linen106-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Career_overview">Career overview</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linda_Ronstadt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Career overview">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_influences">Early influences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linda_Ronstadt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Early influences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:25%; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>"I don't record (any type of genre of music) that I didn't hear in my family's living room by the time I was 10. It just is my rule that I don't break because ... I can't do it authentically ... I really think that you're just hard-wiring (synapses) in your brain up until the age of maybe 12 or 10, and there are certain things you can't learn in an authentic way after that."</p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">—Linda Ronstadt<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Asheralbumsales_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Asheralbumsales-48">[48]</a></sup></cite></div>
<p>Ronstadt's early family life was filled with music and tradition, which influenced the stylistic and musical choices she later made in her career. Growing up, she listened to many types of music, including <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Mexico" title="Music of Mexico">Mexican music</a>, which was sung by her entire family and was a staple in her childhood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AARP-07_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-AARP-07-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>Ronstadt has remarked that everything she has recorded on her own records – rock 'n' roll, jazz, rhythm and blues, gospel, opera, country, choral, and mariachi – is all music she heard her family sing in their living room, or heard played on the radio, by the age of 10. She credits her mother for her appreciation of <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_and_Sullivan" title="Gilbert and Sullivan">Gilbert and Sullivan</a> and her father for introducing her to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Traditional_pop" title="Traditional pop">traditional pop</a> and <a href="/wiki/Great_American_Songbook" title="Great American Songbook">Great American Songbook</a> repertoire that she would, in turn, help reintroduce to an entire generation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Asheralbumsales_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-Asheralbumsales-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-starbulletin_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-starbulletin-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: left; clear: left; margin: 0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0; width:200px; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>"If I didn't hear it on the radio, or if my dad wasn't playing it on the piano, or if my brother wasn't playing it on the guitar or singing it in his boys' choir, or my mother and sister weren't practicing a Broadway tune or a Gilbert and Sullivan song, then I can't do it today. It's as simple as that. All of my influences and my authenticity are a direct result of the music played in that Tucson living room."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McGrath03_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-McGrath03-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">—Linda Rondstadt</cite></div>
<p>Early on, her singing style had been influenced by singers such as <a href="/wiki/Lola_Beltr%C3%A1n" title="Lola Beltrán">Lola Beltrán</a> and <a href="/wiki/%C3%89dith_Piaf" title="Édith Piaf">Édith Piaf</a>; she has called their singing and rhythms "more like Greek music ... It's sort of like 6/8 <a href="/wiki/Time_signature" title="Time signature">time signature</a> ... very hard driving and very intense."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-influences_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-influences-52">[52]</a></sup> She also drew influence from country singer <a href="/wiki/Hank_Williams" title="Hank Williams">Hank Williams</a>.</p>
<p>She has said that "all girl singers" eventually "have to curtsy to <a href="/wiki/Ella_Fitzgerald" title="Ella Fitzgerald">Ella Fitzgerald</a> and <a href="/wiki/Billie_Holiday" title="Billie Holiday">Billie Holiday</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tiptheirhat_29-3"><a href="#cite_note-tiptheirhat-29">[29]</a></sup> Of <a href="/wiki/Maria_Callas" title="Maria Callas">Maria Callas</a>, Ronstadt says, "There's no one in her league. That's it. Period. I learn more ... about singing rock n roll from listening to Maria Callas records than I ever would from listening to pop music for a month of Sundays. ... She's the greatest chick singer ever."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTGarden_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYTGarden-53">[53]</a></sup> She admires Callas for her musicianship and her attempts to push 20th-century singing, particularly opera, back into the <a href="/wiki/Bel_canto" title="Bel canto">bel canto</a> "natural style of singing".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kqed_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-kqed-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>A self-described product of American radio of the 1950s and 1960s, Ronstadt is a fan of its eclectic and diverse music programming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-starbulletin_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-starbulletin-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Beginning_of_professional_career">Beginning of professional career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linda_Ronstadt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Beginning of professional career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>At age 14, Ronstadt formed a folk trio with her brother Peter and sister Suzy. The group played coffeehouses, fraternity houses, and other small venues, billing themselves as "the Union City Ramblers" and "the Three Ronstadts", and they even recorded themselves at a Tucson studio under the name "the New Union Ramblers".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MIXMag2000_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-MIXMag2000-55">[55]</a></sup> Their repertoire included the music they grew up on – folk, country, bluegrass, and Mexican.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ramblers_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-ramblers-56">[56]</a></sup> But increasingly, Ronstadt wanted to make a union of folk music and rock 'n' roll,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hamill_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-hamill-40">[40]</a></sup> and in 1964, after a semester at Arizona State University,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ramblers_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-ramblers-56">[56]</a></sup> the 18-year-old decided to move to Los Angeles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LATimes_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-LATimes-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Soulful_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Soulful-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Claire_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Claire-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Stone_Poneys">The Stone Poneys</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linda_Ronstadt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: The Stone Poneys">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Stone_Poneys" title="Stone Poneys">Stone Poneys</a></div>
<p>Ronstadt visited a friend from Tucson, <a href="/wiki/Bobby_Kimmel" title="Bobby Kimmel">Bobby Kimmel</a>, in Los Angeles during Easter break from college in 1964, and later that year, shortly before her eighteenth birthday,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LATimes_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-LATimes-57">[57]</a></sup> decided to move there permanently to form a band with him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Soulful_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-Soulful-58">[58]</a></sup> Kimmel had already begun co-writing folk-rock songs with guitarist-songwriter <a href="/wiki/Kenny_Edwards" title="Kenny Edwards">Kenny Edwards</a>, and eventually the three of them were signed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nik_Venet" title="Nik Venet">Nik Venet</a> to <a href="/wiki/Capitol_Records" title="Capitol Records">Capitol</a> in the summer of 1966 as "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Stone_Poneys" title="The Stone Poneys">the Stone Poneys</a>". The trio released three albums in a 15-month period in 1967–68: <i><a href="/wiki/The_Stone_Poneys_(album)" title="The Stone Poneys (album)">The Stone Poneys</a></i>; <i><a href="/wiki/Evergreen,_Volume_2" title="Evergreen, Volume 2">Evergreen, Volume 2</a></i>; and <i><a href="/wiki/Linda_Ronstadt,_Stone_Poneys_and_Friends,_Vol._III" title="Linda Ronstadt, Stone Poneys and Friends, Vol. III">Linda Ronstadt, Stone Poneys and Friends, Vol. III</a></i>. The band is best known for their hit single "<a href="/wiki/Different_Drum" title="Different Drum">Different Drum</a>" (written by <a href="/wiki/Michael_Nesmith" title="Michael Nesmith">Michael Nesmith</a> prior to his joining <a href="/wiki/The_Monkees" title="The Monkees">the Monkees</a>), which reached number 13 on the <a href="/wiki/Billboard_Hot_100" title="Billboard Hot 100"><i>Billboard</i> Hot 100</a> chart as well as number 12 in <i><a href="/wiki/Cashbox_(magazine)" title="Cashbox (magazine)">Cashbox</a></i> magazine. Nearly 50 years later, the song remains one of Ronstadt's most popular recordings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2008 Australia's <a href="/wiki/Raven_Records" title="Raven Records">Raven Records</a> released a compilation CD titled <i>The Stone Poneys</i>. The disc features all tracks from the first two Stone Poneys albums and four tracks from the third album.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Solo_career">Solo career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linda_Ronstadt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Solo career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Still contractually obligated to Capitol Records, Ronstadt released her first solo album, <i>Hand Sown ... Home Grown</i>, in 1969. It has been called the first <a href="/wiki/Alternative_country" title="Alternative country">alternative country</a> record by a female recording artist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Linen106_37-3"><a href="#cite_note-Linen106-37">[37]</a></sup> During this same period, she contributed to the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Music_From_Free_Creek" title="Music From Free Creek">Music From Free Creek</a></i> "super session" project.</p>
<p>Ronstadt provided the vocals for some commercials during this period, including one for <a href="/wiki/Remington_Products" title="Remington Products">Remington</a> electric razors, in which a multitracked Ronstadt and <a href="/wiki/Frank_Zappa" title="Frank Zappa">Frank Zappa</a> claimed that the electric razor "cleans you, thrills you ... may even keep you from getting busted".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Remington_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Remington-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>Ronstadt's second solo album, <i><a href="/wiki/Silk_Purse_(Linda_Ronstadt_album)" title="Silk Purse (Linda Ronstadt album)">Silk Purse</a></i>, was released in March 1970. Recorded entirely in Nashville, it was produced by <a href="/wiki/Elliot_Mazer" title="Elliot Mazer">Elliot Mazer</a>, whom Ronstadt chose on the advice of <a href="/wiki/Janis_Joplin" title="Janis Joplin">Janis Joplin</a>, who had worked with him on her <i>Cheap Thrills</i> album.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Capitol_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Capitol-62">[62]</a></sup> The <i>Silk Purse</i> album cover showed Ronstadt in a muddy pigpen, while the back and inside cover depicted her onstage wearing bright red. Ronstadt has stated that she was not pleased with the album, although it provided her with her first solo hit, the multi-format single "<a href="/wiki/Long,_Long_Time" title="Long, Long Time">Long, Long Time</a>", and earned her first Grammy nomination (for Best Contemporary Vocal Performance/Female).</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Touring">Touring</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linda_Ronstadt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Touring">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:30%; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>"<a href="/wiki/Judy_Henske" title="Judy Henske">Judy Henske</a>, who was the then reigning queen of folk music, said to me at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Troubadour_(Los_Angeles)" title="The Troubadour (Los Angeles)">The Troubadour</a>, 'Honey, in this town there are four sexes. Men, women, homosexuals, and girl singers.'"</p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">—Linda Ronstadt</cite>
<p><cite style="display: block; text-align: left;"><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Windy_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Windy-63">[63]</a></sup></cite></p>
</div>
<p>In the autumn of 1975, Ronstadt performed shows with <a href="/wiki/Jackson_Browne" title="Jackson Browne">Jackson Browne</a>, The <a href="/wiki/Eagles_(band)" title="Eagles (band)">Eagles</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Toots_and_the_Maytals" title="Toots and the Maytals">Toots and the Maytals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>In a 1976 <i><a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone" title="Rolling Stone">Rolling Stone</a></i> interview with <a href="/wiki/Cameron_Crowe" title="Cameron Crowe">Cameron Crowe</a>, Ronstadt explained that "they haven't invented a word for that loneliness that everybody goes through on the road. The world is tearing by you, real fast, and all these people are looking at you. ... People see me in my 'girl-singer' suit."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> In 1974 she told <a href="/wiki/Peter_Knobler" title="Peter Knobler">Peter Knobler</a> in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crawdaddy!" title="Crawdaddy!">Crawdaddy</a></i>, "People are always taking advantage of you; everybody that's interested in you has got an angle."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>Several years before Ronstadt became what author Gerri Hirshey called the first "arena-class rock diva", with "hugely anticipated tours",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-doubleplatinum_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-doubleplatinum-38">[38]</a></sup> she began her solo career touring the North American concert circuit. But being on the road took its toll both emotionally and professionally. There were few "girl singers" on the rock circuit at the time, and they were relegated to "groupie level when in a crowd of a bunch of rock and roll guys", a status Ronstadt avoided.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-groupie_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-groupie-68">[68]</a></sup> Relating to men on a professional level as fellow musicians led to competition, insecurity, bad romances, and a series of boyfriend-managers. At the time, she admired singers like <a href="/wiki/Maria_Muldaur" title="Maria Muldaur">Maria Muldaur</a> for not sacrificing their femininity, but says she felt enormous self-imposed pressure to compete with "the boys" at every level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Soulful_58-2"><a href="#cite_note-Soulful-58">[58]</a></sup> She noted in a 1969 interview in <i>Fusion</i> magazine that it was difficult being a single "chick singer" with an all-male backup band.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-groupie_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-groupie-68">[68]</a></sup> According to her, it was difficult to get a band of backing musicians because of their ego problem of being labeled sidemen for a female singer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-backupband2_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-backupband2-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>Soon after she went solo in the late 1960s, one of her first backing bands was the pioneering country-rock band <a href="/wiki/Swampwater" title="Swampwater">Swampwater</a>, famous for synthesizing <a href="/wiki/Cajun_music" title="Cajun music">Cajun</a> and swamp-rock elements into their music. Its members included Cajun fiddler <a href="/wiki/Gib_Guilbeau" title="Gib Guilbeau">Gib Guilbeau</a> and John Beland, who later joined <a href="/wiki/The_Flying_Burrito_Brothers" title="The Flying Burrito Brothers">the Flying Burrito Brothers</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-swamprock_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-swamprock-70">[70]</a></sup> as well as Stan Pratt, Thad Maxwell, and Eric White, brother of <a href="/wiki/Clarence_White" title="Clarence White">Clarence White</a> of <a href="/wiki/The_Byrds" title="The Byrds">the Byrds</a>. Swampwater went on to back Ronstadt during TV appearances on <i><a href="/wiki/The_Johnny_Cash_Show_(TV_series)" title="The Johnny Cash Show (TV series)">The Johnny Cash Show</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-swampwater_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-swampwater-71">[71]</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Mike_Douglas_Show" title="The Mike Douglas Show">The Mike Douglas Show</a></i>, and at the Big Sur Folk Festival.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BigSur_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-BigSur-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another backing band featured players <a href="/wiki/Don_Henley" title="Don Henley">Don Henley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glenn_Frey" title="Glenn Frey">Glenn Frey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bernie_Leadon" title="Bernie Leadon">Bernie Leadon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Randy_Meisner" title="Randy Meisner">Randy Meisner</a>, who went on to form the <a href="/wiki/Eagles_(band)" title="Eagles (band)">Eagles</a>. They toured with her for a short period in 1971 and played on <i><a href="/wiki/Linda_Ronstadt_(album)" title="Linda Ronstadt (album)">Linda Ronstadt</a></i>, her self-titled third album, from which the failed single, Ronstadt's version of Browne's "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rock_Me_On_the_Water" title="Rock Me On the Water">Rock Me On the Water</a>", was drawn. At this stage, Ronstadt began working with producer and boyfriend <a href="/wiki/John_Boylan_(record_producer)" title="John Boylan (record producer)">John Boylan</a>. She said, "As soon as I started working with John Boylan, I started co-producing myself. I was always a part of my productions. But I always needed a producer who would carry out my whims."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MIXMag2000_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-MIXMag2000-55">[55]</a></sup> Also in 1971, Ronstadt began talking with <a href="/wiki/David_Geffen" title="David Geffen">David Geffen</a> about moving from Capitol Records to Geffen's <a href="/wiki/Asylum_Records" title="Asylum Records">Asylum Records</a> label.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Collaborations_with_Peter_Asher">Collaborations with Peter Asher</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linda_Ronstadt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Collaborations with Peter Asher">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:25%; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>"In general when you fall in love with an artist and their music, the plan is a fairly simple one. .. get people to go and see them, and make a record that you think properly presents their music to the public and some of which you can get on the radio."</p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">—<a href="/wiki/Peter_Asher" title="Peter Asher">Peter Asher</a>, on collaborating with Ronstadt<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldmine589_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Goldmine589-36">[36]</a></sup></cite></div>
<p>Ronstadt began her fourth solo album, <i><a href="/wiki/Don%27t_Cry_Now" title="Don't Cry Now">Don't Cry Now</a></i>, in 1973, with Boylan (who had negotiated her contract with <a href="/wiki/Asylum_Records" title="Asylum Records">Asylum Records</a>) and <a href="/wiki/J._D._Souther" title="J. D. Souther">John David "J.D." Souther</a> producing most of the album's tracks. But needing someone willing to work with her as an equal, Ronstadt asked <a href="/wiki/Peter_Asher" title="Peter Asher">Peter Asher</a>, who came highly recommended to her by <a href="/wiki/James_Taylor" title="James Taylor">James Taylor's</a> sister <a href="/wiki/Kate_Taylor" title="Kate Taylor">Kate Taylor</a>, to help produce two of them: "Sail Away" and "I Believe in You".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-producer3_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-producer3-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>The album featured Ronstadt's first country hit, "<a href="/wiki/Silver_Threads_and_Golden_Needles" title="Silver Threads and Golden Needles">Silver Threads and Golden Needles</a>", which she had first recorded on <i>Hand Sown ... Home Grown</i> – this time hitting the Country Top 20.</p>
<p>With the release of <i>Don't Cry Now</i>, Ronstadt took on her biggest gig to date as the opening act on <a href="/wiki/Neil_Young" title="Neil Young">Neil Young's</a> <i>Time Fades Away</i> tour, playing for larger crowds than ever before. Backstage at a concert in Texas, <a href="/wiki/Chris_Hillman" title="Chris Hillman">Chris Hillman</a> introduced her to <a href="/wiki/Emmylou_Harris" title="Emmylou Harris">Emmylou Harris</a>, telling them, "You two could be good friends",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goldmine_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-goldmine-75">[75]</a></sup> which soon occurred, resulting in frequent collaborations over the following years. Meanwhile, the album became Ronstadt's most successful up to that time, selling 300,000 copies by the end of 1974.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-producer3_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-producer3-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>Asher turned out to be more collaborative, and more on the same page with her musically, than any producer she had worked with previously.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MIXMag2000_55-2"><a href="#cite_note-MIXMag2000-55">[55]</a></sup> Ronstadt's professional relationship with Asher allowed her to take command and effectively delegate responsibilities in the recording studio.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-producer3_74-2"><a href="#cite_note-producer3-74">[74]</a></sup> Although hesitant at first to work with her because of her reputation for being a "woman of strong opinions (who) knew what she wanted to do (with her career)", he nonetheless agreed to become her full-time producer,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-negativequalities_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-negativequalities-76">[76]</a></sup> and remained in that role through the late 1980s. Asher attributed the long-term success of his working relationship with Ronstadt to the fact that he was the first person to manage and produce her with whom there was a solely professional relationship. "It must be a lot harder to have objective conversations about someone's career when it's someone you sleep with", he said.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-producer3_74-3"><a href="#cite_note-producer3-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>Asher executive produced a tribute CD called <i><a href="/wiki/Listen_to_Me:_Buddy_Holly" title="Listen to Me: Buddy Holly">Listen to Me: Buddy Holly</a></i>, released September 6, 2011, on which Ronstadt's 1976 version of <a href="/wiki/Buddy_Holly" title="Buddy Holly">Buddy Holly's</a> "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/That%27ll_Be_The_Day" title="That'll Be The Day">That'll Be The Day</a>" appears among newly recorded versions of Holly's songs by various artists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Vocal_styles">Vocal styles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linda_Ronstadt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Vocal styles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: left; clear: left; margin: 0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0; width:25%; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>"I grew up singing Mexican music, and that's based on indigenous Mexican rhythms. Mexican music also has an overlay of West African music, based on huapango drums, and it's kind of like a 6/8 <a href="/wiki/Time_signature" title="Time signature">time signature</a>, but it really is a very syncopated 6/8. And that's how I attack vocals."</p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">—Linda Ronstadt, on reconciling her musical instincts with rock 'n' roll.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MIXMag2000_55-3"><a href="#cite_note-MIXMag2000-55">[55]</a></sup></cite></div>
<p>Ronstadt captured the sounds of <a href="/wiki/Country_music" title="Country music">country music</a> and the rhythms of <a href="/wiki/Ranchera" title="Ranchera">ranchera</a> music – which she likened in 1968 to <i>"Mexican bluegrass"</i> – and redirected them into her rock 'n' roll and some of her pop music. Many of these rhythms and sounds were part of her <a href="/wiki/Southwestern_United_States" title="Southwestern United States">Southwestern</a> roots.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Soulful2_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Soulful2-78">[78]</a></sup> Likewise, a country sound and style, a fusion of country music and rock 'n' roll called <a href="/wiki/Country_rock" title="Country rock">Country rock</a>, started to exert its influence on mainstream pop music around the late 1960s, and it became an emerging movement Ronstadt helped form and commercialize. However, as early as 1970, Ronstadt was being criticized by music "purists" for her "brand of music" which crossed many genres. <i>Country Western Stars</i> magazine wrote in 1970 that "Rock people thought she was too gentle, folk people thought she was too pop, and pop people didn't quite understand where she was at, but Country people really loved Linda." She never categorized herself and stuck to her genre-crossing brand of music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CWS_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-CWS-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Interpretive_singer">Interpretive singer</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linda_Ronstadt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Interpretive singer">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Ronstadt is considered an "interpreter of her times",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-interpreter4_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-interpreter4-80">[80]</a></sup> and has earned praise for her courage to put her own unique "stamp" on many of her songs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DeanofRock_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-DeanofRock-81">[81]</a></sup> Nevertheless, her hits were criticized in some quarters for being <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cover_song" title="Cover song">cover songs</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (October 2016)">by whom?</span></a></i>]</sup> Ronstadt herself has indicated that some of her 70s hits were recorded under considerable pressure to create commercially successful recordings, and that she prefers many of her songs that were non-hit album tracks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MIXMag2000_55-4"><a href="#cite_note-MIXMag2000-55">[55]</a></sup> An infrequent songwriter, Ronstadt co-composed only three songs over her long career.</p>
<p>Ronstadt's natural vocal range spans several octaves from <a href="/wiki/Contralto" title="Contralto">contralto</a> to <a href="/wiki/Soprano" title="Soprano">soprano</a>, and occasionally she will showcase this entire range within a single work. Ronstadt was the first female artist in popular music history to accumulate four consecutive platinum albums (fourteen certified million selling, to date). As for the singles, <i><a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone" title="Rolling Stone">Rolling Stone</a></i> magazine pointed out that a whole generation, "but for her, might never have heard the work of artists such as <a href="/wiki/Buddy_Holly" title="Buddy Holly">Buddy Holly</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elvis_Costello" title="Elvis Costello">Elvis Costello</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chuck_Berry" title="Chuck Berry">Chuck Berry</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-interpreter6_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-interpreter6-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:25%; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>"Music is meant to lighten your load. By singing it ... you release (the sadness). And release yourself ... an exercise in exorcism. ... You exorcise that emotion ... and diminish sadness and feel joy."</p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">—Linda Ronstadt<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></cite></div>
<p>Others have argued that Ronstadt had the same generational effect with her <a href="/wiki/Great_American_Songbook" title="Great American Songbook">Great American Songbook</a> music, exposing a whole new generation to the music of the 1920s and 1930s – music which, ironically, was pushed aside because of the advent of rock 'n' roll. When interpreting, Ronstadt said she "sticks to what the music demands", in terms of lyrics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Demands1_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Demands1-84">[84]</a></sup> Explaining that rock and roll music is part of her culture, she says that the songs she sang after her rock and roll hits were part of her soul. "The (Mariachi music) was my father's side of the soul," she was quoted as saying in a 1998 interview she gave at her Tucson home. "My mother's side of my soul was the Nelson Riddle stuff. And I had to do them both in order to reestablish who I was."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hersoul_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-hersoul-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the 1974 book <i>Rock 'N' Roll Woman</i>, author Katherine Orloff writes that Ronstadt's "own musical preferences run strongly to rhythm and blues, the type of music she most frequently chooses to listen to ... (and) her goal is to ... be soulful too. With this in mind, Ronstadt fuses country and rock into a special union."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Soulful_58-3"><a href="#cite_note-Soulful-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>By this stage of her career, Ronstadt had established her niche in the field of country-rock. Along with other musicians such as <a href="/wiki/The_Flying_Burrito_Brothers" title="The Flying Burrito Brothers">the Flying Burrito Brothers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emmylou_Harris" title="Emmylou Harris">Emmylou Harris</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gram_Parsons" title="Gram Parsons">Gram Parsons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Swampwater" title="Swampwater">Swampwater</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neil_Young" title="Neil Young">Neil Young</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Eagles_(band)" title="Eagles (band)">Eagles</a>, she helped free country music from stereotypes and showed rockers that country was okay. However, she stated that she was being pushed hard into singing more rock and roll.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goldmine_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-goldmine-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Most_successful_female_singer_of_the_1970s">Most successful female singer of the 1970s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linda_Ronstadt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Most successful female singer of the 1970s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Author <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Greeley" title="Andrew Greeley">Andrew Greeley</a>, in his book <i>God in Popular Culture</i>, described Ronstadt as "the most successful and certainly the most durable and most gifted woman Rock singer of her era."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greeley_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greeley-86">[86]</a></sup> Signaling her wide popularity as a concert artist, outside of the singles charts and the recording studio, <i><a href="/wiki/Dirty_Linen" title="Dirty Linen">Dirty Linen</a></i> magazine describes her as the "first true woman rock 'n' roll superstar ... (selling) out stadiums with a string of mega-successful albums."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Linen106_37-4"><a href="#cite_note-Linen106-37">[37]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Amazon.com" title="Amazon.com">Amazon.com</a> defines her as <i>the</i> American female rock superstar of the decade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-medecade_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-medecade-87">[87]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Cashbox_(magazine)" title="Cashbox (magazine)">Cashbox</a></i> gave Ronstadt a <i>Special Decade Award</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cashbox_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-cashbox-88">[88]</a></sup> as the top-selling female singer of the 1970s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tiptheirhat_29-4"><a href="#cite_note-tiptheirhat-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>Her album covers, posters, magazine covers – her entire rock 'n' roll image – were as famous as her music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldmine589_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-Goldmine589-36">[36]</a></sup> By the end of the decade, the singer whom the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chicago_Sun_Times" title="Chicago Sun Times">Chicago Sun Times</a></i> described as the "Dean of the 1970s school of female rock singers"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DeanofRock_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-DeanofRock-81">[81]</a></sup> became what <i><a href="/wiki/Redbook" title="Redbook">Redbook</a></i> called "the most successful female rock star in the world."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Redbook_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Redbook-89">[89]</a></sup> "Female" was the important qualifier, according to <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i> magazine, which labeled her "a rarity ... to (have survived) ... in the shark-infested deeps of rock."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Time_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Time-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although Ronstadt had been a cult favorite on the music scene for several years, 1975 was "remembered in the music biz as the year when 29-year-old Linda Ronstadt <i>belatedly</i> happened."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-people75_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-people75-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<p>With the release of <i>Heart Like a Wheel</i>‍—‌named after one of the album's songs, written by <a href="/wiki/Anna_McGarrigle" title="Anna McGarrigle">Anna McGarrigle</a>‍—‌Ronstadt reached number 1 on the <a href="/wiki/Billboard_200" title="Billboard 200"><i>Billboard</i> 200</a> chart;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BillboardWheelno1on200_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-BillboardWheelno1on200-92">[92]</a></sup> it was also the first of four number 1 Country Albums, and the disc was certified double-platinum<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RIAAsearch_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-RIAAsearch-93">[93]</a></sup> (over two million copies sold in the U.S.). In many instances, her own interpretations were more successful than the original recordings, and many times new songwriters were discovered by a larger audience as a result of her interpretation and recording. Ronstadt had major success interpreting songs from a diverse spectrum of artists.</p>
<p><i>Heart Like a Wheel</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s first single release, "<a href="/wiki/You%27re_No_Good" title="You're No Good">You're No Good</a>" – a rockified version of an R&amp;B song written by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clint_Ballard,_Jr." title="Clint Ballard, Jr.">Clint Ballard, Jr.</a> that Ronstadt had initially resisted because <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Gold" title="Andrew Gold">Andrew Gold's</a> guitar tracks sounded too much like a "Beatles song" to her<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-producer3_74-4"><a href="#cite_note-producer3-74">[74]</a></sup> – climbed to number 1 on both the <i><a href="/wiki/Billboard_(magazine)" title="Billboard (magazine)">Billboard</a></i> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cash_Box" title="Cash Box">Cash Box</a> Pop singles charts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bronson_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-bronson-94">[94]</a></sup> The album's second single release, "<a href="/wiki/When_Will_I_Be_Loved_(song)" title="When Will I Be Loved (song)">When Will I Be Loved</a>" – an uptempo country-rock version of a Top 10 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Everly_Brothers" title="Everly Brothers">Everly Brothers</a> song – hit number 1 in <i>Cashbox</i> and number 2 in <i>Billboard</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bronson_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-bronson-94">[94]</a></sup> The song was also Ronstadt's first number 1 country hit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bronson_94-2"><a href="#cite_note-bronson-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<p>The album showed a physically attractive Ronstadt on the cover but, more importantly, its critical and commercial success was due to a fine presentation of country and rock, with <i>Heart Like a Wheel</i> her first of many major commercial successes that would set her on the path to being one of the best-selling female artists of all time. Ronstadt won her first Grammy Award<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1stgrammy_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-1stgrammy-95">[95]</a></sup> for <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Award_for_Best_Female_Country_Vocal_Performance" title="Grammy Award for Best Female Country Vocal Performance">Best Country Vocal Performance/Female</a> for "I Can't Help It (If I'm Still In Love With You)" which was originally a 1940s hit by <a href="/wiki/Hank_Williams" title="Hank Williams">Hank Williams</a>. Ronstadt's interpretation peaked at number 2 on the country chart. The album itself was nominated for the Album of the Year Grammy.</p>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone" title="Rolling Stone">Rolling Stone</a></i> magazine put Ronstadt on its cover in March 1975. It was the first of six <i>Rolling Stone</i> magazine covers shot by photographer <a href="/wiki/Annie_Leibovitz" title="Annie Leibovitz">Annie Leibovitz</a>. It included her as the featured artist with a full photo layout and an article by <a href="/wiki/Ben_Fong-Torres" title="Ben Fong-Torres">Ben Fong-Torres</a>, discussing Ronstadt's many struggling years in rock n roll, as well as her home life and what it was like to be a woman on tour in a decidedly all-male environment.</p>
<p>In September 1975, Ronstadt's album <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prisoner_In_Disguise" title="Prisoner In Disguise">Prisoner In Disguise</a></i> was released. It quickly climbed into the Top Five on the <i>Billboard</i> Album Chart and sold over a million copies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RIAAsearch_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-RIAAsearch-93">[93]</a></sup> It became her second in a row to go platinum, "a grand slam" in the same year (Ronstadt would eventually become the first female artist in popular music history to have three consecutive platinum albums and would ultimately go on to have eight consecutive platinum albums, and then another six between 1983 and 1990).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-people75_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-people75-91">[91]</a></sup> The disc's first single release was "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Love_Is_A_Rose" title="Love Is A Rose">Love Is A Rose</a>". It was climbing the pop and country charts but <a href="/wiki/Heat_Wave_(Martha_and_the_Vandellas_song)" title="Heat Wave (Martha and the Vandellas song)">Heat Wave</a>, a rockified version of the 1963 hit by <a href="/wiki/Martha_and_the_Vandellas" title="Martha and the Vandellas">Martha and the Vandellas</a>, was receiving considerable airplay. Asylum pulled the "Love Is a Rose" single and issued "Heat Wave" with "Love Is a Rose" on the B-side. "Heat Wave" hit the Top Five on <i>Billboard'</i>s Hot 100 while "Love Is A Rose" hit the Top Five on Billboard's country chart.</p>
<p>In 1976, Ronstadt reached the Top 3 of <i>Billboard</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s Album Chart and won her second career <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Award_for_Best_Female_Pop_Vocal_Performance" title="Grammy Award for Best Female Pop Vocal Performance">Grammy Award for Best Female Pop Vocal Performance</a> for her third consecutive platinum<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RIAAsearch_93-2"><a href="#cite_note-RIAAsearch-93">[93]</a></sup> album <i><a href="/wiki/Hasten_Down_the_Wind" title="Hasten Down the Wind">Hasten Down the Wind</a></i>. The album featured a sexy, revealing cover shot and showcased Ronstadt the singer-songwriter, who composed two of its songs, "Try Me Again" (co-authored with Andrew Gold) and "Lo Siento Mi Vida". It also included an interpretation of Willie Nelson's classic "Crazy", which became a Top 10 Country hit for Ronstadt in early 1977.</p>
<p>At the end of 1977, Ronstadt surpassed the success of <i>Heart Like a Wheel</i> with her album <i><a href="/wiki/Simple_Dreams" title="Simple Dreams">Simple Dreams</a></i>, which held the number 1 position for five consecutive weeks on the <i>Billboard</i> 200 chart.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Billboardsimpledreams5weeks_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Billboardsimpledreams5weeks-96">[96]</a></sup> It sold over 3½ million copies in less than a year in the U.S. alone – a record for a female artist. <i>Simple Dreams</i> spawned a string of hit singles on numerous charts. Among them were the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RIAA" title="RIAA">RIAA</a> platinum-certified single "<a href="/wiki/Blue_Bayou" title="Blue Bayou">Blue Bayou</a>", a country rock interpretation of a <a href="/wiki/Roy_Orbison" title="Roy Orbison">Roy Orbison</a> song; "It's So Easy" – previously sung by Buddy Holly – ; and "<a href="/wiki/Poor_Poor_Pitiful_Me" title="Poor Poor Pitiful Me">Poor Poor Pitiful Me</a>", a song written by <a href="/wiki/Warren_Zevon" title="Warren Zevon">Warren Zevon</a>, an up-and-coming songwriter of the time. The album garnered several Grammy Award nominations – including Record of the Year and Best Pop Vocal Performance/Female for "Blue Bayou" – and won its art director, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kosh_(art_director)" title="Kosh (art director)">Kosh</a>, a Grammy Award for Best Album Cover, the first of three Grammy Awards he would win for designing Ronstadt album covers.</p>
<p><i>Simple Dreams</i> became one of the singer's best-selling international-selling albums as well, reaching number 1 on the Australian and Canadian Pop and Country Albums charts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-international_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-international-97">[97]</a></sup> <i>Simple Dreams</i> also made Ronstadt the most successful international female touring artist as well. The same year, she completed a concert tour around Europe. As <i>Country Music Magazine</i> wrote in October 1978, <i>Simple Dreams</i> solidified Ronstadt's role as "easily the most successful female rock and roll <i>and</i> country star at this time."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ramblers_56-2"><a href="#cite_note-ramblers-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>Also in 1977, she was asked by the <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Dodgers" title="Los Angeles Dodgers">Los Angeles Dodgers</a> to sing the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._National_Anthem" title="U.S. National Anthem">U.S. National Anthem</a> at game three of the <a href="/wiki/World_Series" title="World Series">World Series</a> against the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Yankees" title="New York Yankees">New York Yankees</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nationalanthem_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-nationalanthem-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Time_magazine_and_Ronstadt.27s_.22rock_chick.22_image"><i>Time</i> magazine and Ronstadt's "rock chick" image</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linda_Ronstadt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Time magazine and Ronstadt's &quot;rock chick&quot; image">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Ronstadt has remarked that she felt as though she was "artificially encouraged to kinda cop a really tough attitude (and be tough) because rock and roll is kind of tough (business)," which she felt wasn't worn quite authentically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-identitycrisis_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-identitycrisis-99">[99]</a></sup> Female rock artists like her and <a href="/wiki/Janis_Joplin" title="Janis Joplin">Janis Joplin</a>, whom she described as lovely, shy, and very literate in real life and the antithesis of the "red hot mamma" she was artificially encouraged to project, went through an identity crisis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-identitycrisis_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-identitycrisis-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RonstadtTime.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="415" data-file-width="310" height="295" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/38/RonstadtTime.jpg/220px-RonstadtTime.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/3/38/RonstadtTime.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RonstadtTime.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
On the cover of the February 28, 1977, issue of <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i> magazine</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>By the mid-1970s, Ronstadt's image became just as famous as her music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldmine589_36-3"><a href="#cite_note-Goldmine589-36">[36]</a></sup> In 1976 and 1977, she appeared on the covers of <i><a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone" title="Rolling Stone">Rolling Stone</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i>, respectively. The <i>Rolling Stone</i> cover story was accompanied by a series of photographs of Ronstadt in a skimpy red slip, taken by <a href="/wiki/Annie_Leibovitz" title="Annie Leibovitz">Annie Leibovitz</a>. Ronstadt felt deceived by the photographer, not realizing that the photos would be so revealing. She says her manager Peter Asher kicked Leibovitz out of the house when she visited to show them the photographs prior to publication. Leibovitz had refused to let them veto any of the photos, which included one of Ronstadt sprawled across a bed in her underpants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldmine589_36-4"><a href="#cite_note-Goldmine589-36">[36]</a></sup> In a 1977 interview, Ronstadt explained, "Annie [Leibovitz] saw that picture as an expose of my personality. She was right. But I wouldn't choose to show a picture like that to anybody who didn't know me personally, because only friends could get the other sides of me in balance."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<p>Her 1977 appearance on the cover of <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i> magazine under the banner "<a href="/wiki/Torch_song" title="Torch song">Torchy</a> Rock" was also upsetting to Ronstadt, considering what the image appeared to project about the most famous woman in rock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-identitycrisis_99-2"><a href="#cite_note-identitycrisis-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rockwell_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-rockwell-101">[101]</a></sup> At a time in the industry when men still told women what to sing and what to wear,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Asherscomment_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Asherscomment-102">[102]</a></sup> Ronstadt hated the image of her that was projected to the world on that cover,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-identitycrisis_99-3"><a href="#cite_note-identitycrisis-99">[99]</a></sup> and she noted recently how the photographer kept forcing her to wear a dress, which was an image she did not want to project.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-identitycrisis_99-4"><a href="#cite_note-identitycrisis-99">[99]</a></sup> In 2004, she was interviewed for <i><a href="/wiki/CBS_This_Morning" title="CBS This Morning">CBS This Morning</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cbs_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-cbs-103">[103]</a></sup> and stated that this image was not her because she did not sit like that. Asher noted, "Anyone who's met Linda for 10 seconds will know that I couldn't possibly have been her <a href="/wiki/Svengali" title="Svengali">Svengali</a>. She's an extremely determined woman, in every area. To me, she was everything that feminism's about."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Asherscomment_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-Asherscomment-102">[102]</a></sup> Qualities which, Asher has stated, were considered a "negative (in a woman at that time), whereas in a man they were perceived as being masterful and bold".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-negativequalities_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-negativequalities-76">[76]</a></sup> Since her solo career began, Ronstadt has fought hard to be recognized as a solo female singer in the world of rock, and her portrayal on the <i>Time</i> cover did not appear to help the situation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-backupband2_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-backupband2-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1978, <i>Rolling Stone</i> magazine declared Ronstadt, "by far America's best-known female rock singer."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RocksVenusSuccess_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-RocksVenusSuccess-39">[39]</a></sup> She scored a third number 1 album on the Billboard Album Chart – at this point equaling the record set by Carole King in 1974 – with <i><a href="/wiki/Living_in_the_USA" title="Living in the USA">Living in the USA</a></i>. She achieved a major hit single with "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ooh_Baby_Baby" title="Ooh Baby Baby">Ooh Baby Baby</a>", with her rendition hitting all four major singles charts (Pop, AC, Country, R&amp;B). <i>Living in the USA</i> was the first album by any recording act in music history to ship double-platinum (over 2 million advance copies).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-doubleplatinum_38-2"><a href="#cite_note-doubleplatinum-38">[38]</a></sup> The album eventually sold 3 million U.S. copies.</p>
<p>At the end of that year, <i>Billboard</i> magazine crowned Ronstadt with three number-one Awards for the Year: Pop Female Singles Artist of the Year, Pop Female Album Artist of the Year, and Female Artist of the Year (overall).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TopFemale_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-TopFemale-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>Living in the USA</i> showed the singer on roller skates with a newly short, permed hairdo on the album cover. Ronstadt continued this theme on concert tour promotional posters with photos of her on roller skates in a dramatic pose with a large American flag in the background. By this stage of her career, she was using posters to promote every album<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldmine589_36-5"><a href="#cite_note-Goldmine589-36">[36]</a></sup> and concert – which at the time were recorded live on radio or television.</p>
<p>Ronstadt was also featured in the 1978 film <i><a href="/wiki/FM_(film)" title="FM (film)">FM</a></i>, where the plot involved disc jockeys attempting to broadcast a Ronstadt concert live, without a competing station's knowledge. The film also showed Ronstadt performing the songs "Poor, Poor Pitiful Me", "Love Me Tender", and "<a href="/wiki/Tumbling_Dice" title="Tumbling Dice">Tumbling Dice</a>". Ronstadt was persuaded to record "Tumbling Dice" after <a href="/wiki/Mick_Jagger" title="Mick Jagger">Mick Jagger</a> came backstage when she was at a concert and said, "You do too many ballads, you should do more rock and roll songs."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MickJagger_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-MickJagger-105">[105]</a></sup></p>
<p>Following the success of <i>Living in the USA</i>, Ronstadt conducted album promotional tours and concerts. She made a guest appearance onstage with <a href="/wiki/The_Rolling_Stones" title="The Rolling Stones">the Rolling Stones</a> at the Tucson Community Center on July 21, 1978, in her hometown of Tucson, where she and Jagger sang "Tumbling Dice".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> On singing with Jagger, Ronstadt later said, "I loved it. I didn't have a trace of stage fright. I'm scared to death all the way through my own shows. But it was too much fun to get scared. He's so silly onstage, he knocks you over. I mean you have to be on your toes or you wind up falling on your face."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RocksVenusSuccess_39-2"><a href="#cite_note-RocksVenusSuccess-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Highest-paid_woman_in_rock">Highest-paid woman in rock</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linda_Ronstadt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Highest-paid woman in rock">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:25%; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>"Rock is the thumping heart of Linda's music, and the rock world is dominated by males. The biggest stars are male, and so are the back-up musicians ... rock beats are ... phallic, and lyrics ... masculine. ... <a href="/wiki/Janis_Joplin" title="Janis Joplin">Janis Joplin</a>, the first great white woman rocker, rattled the bars ... but she died. ... <a href="/wiki/Joni_Mitchell" title="Joni Mitchell">Joni Mitchell</a> ... stylish (but can't) compete in drawing power with men ... (however) Linda Ronstadt ... has made herself one of the biggest individual rock draws in the world."</p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">—<i>Time</i> magazine, in 1977<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Time_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-Time-90">[90]</a></sup></cite></div>
<p>By the end of 1978, Ronstadt had solidified her role as one of rock and pop's most successful solo female acts, and owing to her consistent platinum album success, and her ability as the first-ever woman to sell out concerts in arenas and stadiums hosting tens of thousands of fans,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tiptheirhat_29-5"><a href="#cite_note-tiptheirhat-29">[29]</a></sup> Ronstadt became the "highest paid woman in rock".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-doubleplatinum_38-3"><a href="#cite_note-doubleplatinum-38">[38]</a></sup> She had six platinum-certified albums, three of which were number 1 on the <i>Billboard</i> album chart, and numerous charted pop singles. In 1978 alone, she made over $12 million<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tiptheirhat_29-6"><a href="#cite_note-tiptheirhat-29">[29]</a></sup> (equivalent to $44,000,000 in 2016 dollars)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CPI1_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-CPI1-109">[109]</a></sup> and in the same year her albums sales were reported to be 17 million – grossing over $60 million<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ward1978_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ward1978-110">[110]</a></sup> (equivalent to a gross of over $220,000,000, in 2016 dollars).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CPI1_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-CPI1-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>As <i>Rolling Stone</i> magazine dubbed her "Rock's Venus",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RocksVenusSuccess_39-3"><a href="#cite_note-RocksVenusSuccess-39">[39]</a></sup> her record sales continued to multiply and set records themselves. By 1979, Ronstadt had collected eight gold, six platinum, and four multi-platinum certifications for her albums, an unprecedented feat at the time. Her 1976 <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greatest_Hits_(Linda_Ronstadt)" title="Greatest Hits (Linda Ronstadt)">Greatest Hits</a></i> album would sell consistently for the next 25 years and in 2001 was certified by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RIAA" title="RIAA">RIAA</a> for seven-times platinum<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RIAAsearch_93-3"><a href="#cite_note-RIAAsearch-93">[93]</a></sup> (over seven million U.S. copies sold). In 1980, <i><a href="/wiki/Greatest_Hits,_Volume_2_(Linda_Ronstadt_album)" title="Greatest Hits, Volume 2 (Linda Ronstadt album)">Greatest Hits, Volume 2</a></i> was released and certified platinum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RIAAsearch_93-4"><a href="#cite_note-RIAAsearch-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1979, Ronstadt went on an international tour, playing in arenas across Australia to Japan, including the <a href="/wiki/Melbourne_Cricket_Ground" title="Melbourne Cricket Ground">Melbourne Cricket Ground</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Melbourne,_Australia" title="Melbourne, Australia">Melbourne, Australia</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Nippon_Budokan" title="Nippon Budokan">Budokan</a> in Tokyo. She also participated in a benefit concert for her friend <a href="/wiki/Lowell_George" title="Lowell George">Lowell George</a>, held at <a href="/wiki/The_Forum_(Inglewood,_California)" title="The Forum (Inglewood, California)">The Forum</a>, in Los Angeles.</p>
<p>By the end of the decade, Ronstadt had outsold her female competition; no other female artist to date had five straight platinum LPs – <i><a href="/wiki/Hasten_Down_the_Wind" title="Hasten Down the Wind">Hasten Down the Wind</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Heart_Like_a_Wheel" title="Heart Like a Wheel">Heart Like a Wheel</a></i> among them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PeopleMagazine_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-PeopleMagazine-111">[111]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Us_Weekly" title="Us Weekly">Us Weekly</a></i> reported in 1978 that Ronstadt, <a href="/wiki/Joni_Mitchell" title="Joni Mitchell">Joni Mitchell</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stevie_Nicks" title="Stevie Nicks">Stevie Nicks</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Carly_Simon" title="Carly Simon">Carly Simon</a> had become "The Queens of Rock"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ward1978_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ward1978-110">[110]</a></sup> and "Rock is no longer exclusively male. There is a new royalty ruling today's record charts."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ward1978_110-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ward1978-110">[110]</a></sup></p>
<p>She would go on to parlay her mass commercial appeal with major success in interpreting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Great_American_Songbook" title="The Great American Songbook">The Great American Songbook</a> – made famous a generation before by <a href="/wiki/Frank_Sinatra" title="Frank Sinatra">Frank Sinatra</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ella_Fitzgerald" title="Ella Fitzgerald">Ella Fitzgerald</a> – and later the Mexican folk songs of her childhood.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="From_rock_to_operetta">From rock to operetta</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linda_Ronstadt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: From rock to operetta">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: left; clear: left; margin: 0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>"Rampant eclecticism is my middle name."</p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">—Linda Ronstadt<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Windy_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-Windy-63">[63]</a></sup></cite></div>
<p>In 1980, Ronstadt released <i><a href="/wiki/Mad_Love_(Linda_Ronstadt_album)" title="Mad Love (Linda Ronstadt album)">Mad Love</a></i>, her seventh consecutive platinum-selling album. It was a straightforward rock and roll album with post-punk, new wave influences, including tracks by songwriters such as Elvis Costello, <a href="/wiki/The_Cretones" title="The Cretones">the Cretones</a>, and musician Mark Goldenberg who played on the record himself.</p>
<p>She also made the cover of <i>Rolling Stone</i> magazine for a record-setting sixth time. <i>Mad Love</i> entered the <i>Billboard</i> Album Chart in the Top Five its first week (a record at that time) and climbed to the number 3 position. The project continued her streak of Top 10 hits with "How Do I Make You?", originally recorded by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Billy_Thermal" title="Billy Thermal">Billy Thermal</a>, and "Hurt So Bad", originally a Top 10 hit for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Little_Anthony_%26_the_Imperials" title="Little Anthony &amp; the Imperials">Little Anthony &amp; the Imperials</a>. The album earned Ronstadt a 1980 <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Award" title="Grammy Award">Grammy Award</a> nomination for Best Rock Vocal Performance/Female (although she lost to <a href="/wiki/Pat_Benatar" title="Pat Benatar">Pat Benatar's</a> <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crimes_of_Passion" title="Crimes of Passion">Crimes of Passion</a></i> album). Benatar praised Ronstadt by stating, "There are a lot of good female singers around. How could I be the best? Ronstadt is still alive!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-recordreview_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-recordreview-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pirates-of-penzance-DVDcover.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="475" data-file-width="339" height="308" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/8c/Pirates-of-penzance-DVDcover.jpg/220px-Pirates-of-penzance-DVDcover.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/8c/Pirates-of-penzance-DVDcover.jpg/330px-Pirates-of-penzance-DVDcover.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/8/8c/Pirates-of-penzance-DVDcover.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
